# Title: Quantum Physics I: A Comprehensive Introduction

## Chapter 7: Quantum Physics in Two-dimensional Potentials




### Introduction

Welcome to Chapter 7 of "Quantum Physics I". In this chapter, we will delve into the fascinating world of Angular Momentum and Central Potentials. These two concepts are fundamental to understanding the behavior of particles at the quantum level.

Angular Momentum is a concept that is deeply rooted in classical mechanics. It is a measure of the rotational motion of an object. In quantum mechanics, however, the concept of Angular Momentum takes on a more complex and intriguing form. It is no longer just a measure of rotational motion, but also a fundamental property of particles. The quantum mechanical Angular Momentum is quantized, meaning it can only take on certain discrete values. This is a profound departure from classical mechanics, where Angular Momentum can take on any value.

Central Potentials, on the other hand, are potential energy functions that depend only on the distance from the center of a system. They are crucial in understanding the behavior of particles in systems where the forces acting on the particles depend only on their distance from the center. Examples of such systems include atoms and molecules, where the electrons are held in orbit around the nucleus.

In this chapter, we will explore the mathematical formalism of Angular Momentum and Central Potentials, and how they are used to describe the behavior of particles at the quantum level. We will also discuss the physical implications of these concepts, and how they have revolutionized our understanding of the quantum world.

As always, we will use the powerful language of mathematics to express these concepts. All mathematical expressions will be formatted using the TeX and LaTeX style syntax, rendered using the MathJax library. For example, inline math will be written as `$y_j(n)$`, and equations will be written as `$$
\Delta w = ...
$$`.

So, let's embark on this exciting journey into the quantum world of Angular Momentum and Central Potentials.




### Section: 7.1 Resonances and Breit-Wigner Distribution

#### 7.1a Resonances in Quantum Physics

In quantum physics, resonances are phenomena that occur when a system is driven at a frequency that matches its natural frequency. This results in a large amplitude response, a phenomenon known as resonance. In the context of quantum mechanics, resonances can occur in systems with discrete energy levels, such as atoms or molecules.

The study of resonances is crucial in quantum physics as it provides insights into the behavior of particles at the quantum level. It allows us to understand how particles interact with each other and how they respond to external forces.

One of the most significant applications of resonances in quantum physics is in the study of nuclear reactions. In nuclear physics, resonances are used to explain the behavior of particles in the nucleus of an atom. The Breit-Wigner distribution, named after physicists Gregory Breit and Eugene Wigner, is a mathematical model used to describe resonances in quantum systems.

The Breit-Wigner distribution is given by the equation:

$$
f(E) = \frac{1}{\pi} \frac{\Gamma/2}{(E-E_0)^2 + (\Gamma/2)^2}
$$

where $E$ is the energy, $E_0$ is the resonance energy, and $\Gamma$ is the resonance width. The resonance width is related to the lifetime of the resonance state. A wider resonance (larger $\Gamma$) corresponds to a shorter lifetime.

The Breit-Wigner distribution is often used to describe the distribution of particles in a resonance state. It is particularly useful in nuclear physics, where it is used to describe the distribution of particles in the excited states of the nucleus.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the mathematical formalism of the Breit-Wigner distribution and explore its implications in quantum physics.

#### 7.1b Breit-Wigner Distribution

The Breit-Wigner distribution, named after physicists Gregory Breit and Eugene Wigner, is a mathematical model used to describe resonances in quantum systems. It is particularly useful in nuclear physics, where it is used to describe the distribution of particles in the excited states of the nucleus.

The Breit-Wigner distribution is given by the equation:

$$
f(E) = \frac{1}{\pi} \frac{\Gamma/2}{(E-E_0)^2 + (\Gamma/2)^2}
$$

where $E$ is the energy, $E_0$ is the resonance energy, and $\Gamma$ is the resonance width. The resonance width is related to the lifetime of the resonance state. A wider resonance (larger $\Gamma$) corresponds to a shorter lifetime.

The Breit-Wigner distribution is often used to describe the distribution of particles in a resonance state. It is particularly useful in nuclear physics, where it is used to describe the distribution of particles in the excited states of the nucleus.

The Breit-Wigner distribution is a Lorentzian distribution, which means that it is symmetric about the resonance energy $E_0$. The distribution is also normalized, meaning that the total area under the curve is equal to 1. This is a crucial property of the Breit-Wigner distribution, as it ensures that the total probability of finding a particle in the resonance state is 1.

The Breit-Wigner distribution is often used in conjunction with the concept of resonances in quantum physics. Resonances occur when a system is driven at a frequency that matches its natural frequency, resulting in a large amplitude response. In quantum systems, resonances can occur in systems with discrete energy levels, such as atoms or molecules.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the mathematical formalism of the Breit-Wigner distribution and explore its implications in quantum physics.

#### 7.1c Applications of Resonances and Breit-Wigner Distribution

The Breit-Wigner distribution and the concept of resonances have found numerous applications in quantum physics, particularly in the study of nuclear reactions. The distribution is used to describe the distribution of particles in the excited states of the nucleus, providing insights into the behavior of particles at the quantum level.

One of the most significant applications of the Breit-Wigner distribution is in the study of nuclear reactions. In nuclear physics, resonances are used to explain the behavior of particles in the nucleus of an atom. The Breit-Wigner distribution is particularly useful in this context, as it allows us to describe the distribution of particles in a resonance state.

The Breit-Wigner distribution is also used in the study of particle physics. In particle physics, resonances are used to describe the behavior of particles in the excited states of the nucleus. The distribution is particularly useful in this context, as it allows us to describe the distribution of particles in a resonance state.

The Breit-Wigner distribution is also used in the study of quantum mechanics. In quantum mechanics, resonances are used to explain the behavior of particles in systems with discrete energy levels. The distribution is particularly useful in this context, as it allows us to describe the distribution of particles in a resonance state.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the mathematical formalism of the Breit-Wigner distribution and explore its implications in quantum physics.




#### 7.2a Understanding Central Potentials

In quantum mechanics, central potentials are a class of potential energy functions that depend only on the distance from the center of the system. They are particularly important in the study of atoms and molecules, where the potential energy due to the Coulomb interaction between the electrons and the nucleus depends only on the distance from the nucleus.

The Schrödinger equation for a central potential can be solved exactly, leading to the concept of quantum numbers and the hydrogen atom model. The solutions of the Schrödinger equation for a central potential are spherical harmonics, which describe the angular part of the wave function, and radial functions, which describe the radial part.

The quantum numbers $l$ and $m$ that appear in the spherical harmonics correspond to the angular momentum of the electron. The quantum number $l$ is the azimuthal quantum number, and it determines the shape of the orbital. The quantum number $m$ is the magnetic quantum number, and it determines the orientation of the orbital in space.

The quantum number $n$ that appears in the radial functions corresponds to the principal quantum number. It determines the energy of the electron. The larger the value of $n$, the higher the energy of the electron.

The solutions of the Schrödinger equation for a central potential also lead to the concept of quantum states. A quantum state is a state of a quantum system that is described by a wave function. The wave function provides a complete description of the state of the system.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the mathematical formalism of central potentials and explore their implications in quantum physics.

#### 7.2b Solving the Schrödinger Equation for Central Potentials

The Schrödinger equation for a central potential can be written as:

$$
-\frac{\hbar^2}{2I} \frac{1}{r^2} \frac{d}{dr} \left( r^2 \frac{dR}{dr} \right) + \frac{\hbar^2 l(l+1)}{2I} R = E R
$$

where $I$ is the moment of inertia, $R$ is the radial part of the wave function, $l$ is the azimuthal quantum number, and $E$ is the energy of the system.

The solutions to this equation are spherical harmonics for the angular part and radial functions for the radial part. The spherical harmonics are given by:

$$
Y_l^m(\theta, \phi) = \sqrt{\frac{(2l+1)}{4\pi} \frac{(l-m)!}{(l+m)!} } P_l^m(\cos \theta) e^{im\phi}
$$

where $P_l^m(\cos \theta)$ are the associated Legendre polynomials, and $Y_l^m(\theta, \phi)$ are the spherical harmonics.

The radial functions are given by:

$$
R_{nl}(r) = \sqrt{\left(\frac{2}{na_0}\right)^3 \frac{(n-l-1)!}{2n(n+l)!} } e^{-r/na_0} \left(\frac{2r}{na_0}\right)^l L_{n-l-1}^{2l+1} \left(\frac{2r}{na_0}\right)
$$

where $a_0$ is the Bohr radius, $n$ is the principal quantum number, and $L_{n-l-1}^{2l+1}(x)$ are the associated Laguerre polynomials.

The quantum numbers $l$ and $m$ that appear in the spherical harmonics correspond to the angular momentum of the electron. The quantum number $l$ is the azimuthal quantum number, and it determines the shape of the orbital. The quantum number $m$ is the magnetic quantum number, and it determines the orientation of the orbital in space.

The quantum number $n$ that appears in the radial functions corresponds to the principal quantum number. It determines the energy of the electron. The larger the value of $n$, the higher the energy of the electron.

In the next section, we will explore the physical interpretation of these solutions and their implications for the behavior of quantum systems.

#### 7.2c Applications of Central Potentials

The solutions to the Schrödinger equation for central potentials have numerous applications in quantum physics. They are particularly useful in understanding the behavior of particles in systems with central potentials, such as atoms and molecules.

One of the most significant applications of central potentials is in the study of atomic and molecular systems. The solutions to the Schrödinger equation for central potentials provide a complete description of the quantum states of these systems. This is crucial for understanding the energy levels of atoms and molecules, as well as the transitions between these levels.

For example, the solutions to the Schrödinger equation for the hydrogen atom, which is a system with a central potential, led to the development of the Bohr model of the atom. This model, which describes the electron in the hydrogen atom as moving in circular orbits, was the first successful theory of atomic structure.

In addition to their applications in atomic and molecular systems, solutions to the Schrödinger equation for central potentials are also used in the study of nuclear systems. The potential energy between the protons and neutrons in a nucleus can be approximated as a central potential, and the solutions to the Schrödinger equation for this potential can provide insights into the behavior of particles in the nucleus.

Furthermore, the solutions to the Schrödinger equation for central potentials are used in the study of quantum systems with rotational symmetry, such as the rigid rotor model. This model, which describes a system of particles rotating around a common axis, is used in the study of molecules and other quantum systems.

In conclusion, the solutions to the Schrödinger equation for central potentials have a wide range of applications in quantum physics. They provide a powerful tool for understanding the behavior of particles in various quantum systems, from atoms and molecules to nuclei and rotating systems.




#### 7.3a Algebra of Angular Momentum

The algebra of angular momentum is a fundamental concept in quantum mechanics, particularly in the study of central potentials. It is governed by the commutation relations between the components of the angular momentum operator.

The orbital angular momentum operator is a vector operator, and it can be written in terms of its vector components as $\mathbf{L} = \left(L_x, L_y, L_z\right)$. The components of the angular momentum operator have the following commutation relations with each other:

$$
\left[L_x, L_y\right] = i\hbar L_z, \;\; \left[L_y, L_z\right] = i\hbar L_x, \;\; \left[L_z, L_x\right] = i\hbar L_y
$$

These commutation relations can be generalized to any pair of components $L_l$ and $L_m$:

$$
\left[L_l, L_m\right] = i \hbar \sum_{n=1}^{3} \varepsilon_{lmn} L_n
$$

where "l", "m", "n" are the component indices (1 for "x", 2 for "y", 3 for "z"), and $\varepsilon_{lmn}$ denotes the Levi-Civita symbol.

These commutation relations can be compactly expressed as a vector equation:

$$
\mathbf{L} \times \mathbf{L} = i\hbar \mathbf{L}
$$

These commutation relations are a direct consequence of the canonical commutation relations $[x_l,p_m] = i \hbar \delta_{lm}$, where $\delta_{lm}$ is the Kronecker delta.

In classical physics, there is an analogous relationship:

$$
\left\{L_i, L_j\right\} = \varepsilon_{ijk} L_k
$$

where $L_n$ is a component of the "classical" angular momentum operator, and $\{ ,\}$ is the Poisson bracket.

The same commutation relations apply for the other angular momentum operators (spin and total angular momentum):

$$
\left[S_l, S_m\right] = i \hbar \sum_{n=1}^{3} \varepsilon_{lmn} S_n, \quad \left[J_l, J_m\right] = i \hbar \sum_{n=1}^{3} \varepsilon_{lmn} J_n
$$

These can be assumed to hold in analogy with $L$. Alternatively, they can be derived as discussed below.

These commutation relations mean that $L$ has the mathematical structure of a Lie algebra, and the $\varepsilon_{lmn}$ are its structure constants. In this case, the Lie algebra is SU(2) or SO(3) in physics notation.

#### 7.3b Spin and Total Angular Momentum

In quantum mechanics, the concept of spin is introduced to account for the intrinsic angular momentum of particles. The spin of a particle is a quantum mechanical property that is analogous to the angular momentum, but it is an intrinsic property of the particle, not a result of its motion. The spin of a particle is described by the spin angular momentum operator, denoted as $\mathbf{S}$.

The spin angular momentum operator also obeys the same commutation relations as the orbital angular momentum operator. This means that the spin angular momentum of a particle is also quantized, and it can only take on certain discrete values.

The total angular momentum operator, $\mathbf{J}$, is the sum of the orbital angular momentum operator, $\mathbf{L}$, and the spin angular momentum operator, $\mathbf{S}$. Mathematically, this can be expressed as:

$$
\mathbf{J} = \mathbf{L} + \mathbf{S}
$$

The total angular momentum operator also obeys the same commutation relations as the orbital and spin angular momentum operators. This means that the total angular momentum of a particle is also quantized, and it can only take on certain discrete values.

The commutation relations for the total angular momentum operator can be written as:

$$
\left[J_l, J_m\right] = i \hbar \sum_{n=1}^{3} \varepsilon_{lmn} J_n
$$

where "l", "m", "n" are the component indices (1 for "x", 2 for "y", 3 for "z"), and $\varepsilon_{lmn}$ denotes the Levi-Civita symbol.

These commutation relations are a direct consequence of the canonical commutation relations $[x_l,p_m] = i \hbar \delta_{lm}$, where $\delta_{lm}$ is the Kronecker delta.

In classical physics, there is an analogous relationship for the total angular momentum:

$$
\left\{J_i, J_j\right\} = \varepsilon_{ijk} J_k
$$

where $J_n$ is a component of the "classical" total angular momentum operator, and $\{ ,\}$ is the Poisson bracket.

The same commutation relations apply for the other angular momentum operators (spin and total angular momentum):

$$
\left[S_l, S_m\right] = i \hbar \sum_{n=1}^{3} \varepsilon_{lmn} S_n, \quad \left[J_l, J_m\right] = i \hbar \sum_{n=1}^{3} \varepsilon_{lmn} J_n
$$

These can be assumed to hold in analogy with $L$. Alternatively, they can be derived as discussed below.

These commutation relations mean that $J$ has the mathematical structure of a Lie algebra, and the $\varepsilon_{lmn}$ are its structure constants. In this case, the Lie algebra is SU(2) or SO(3) in physics notation.

#### 7.3c Wigner-Eckart Theorem

The Wigner-Eckart theorem is a fundamental result in quantum mechanics that relates the matrix elements of a spherical tensor operator to the Clebsch-Gordan coefficients. It is named after physicists Eugene Wigner and Carl Eckart, who first derived it.

The theorem is particularly useful in quantum mechanics because it provides a method to calculate the matrix elements of a spherical tensor operator between different angular momentum states. This is important because many physical observables, such as the electric and magnetic moments of a particle, are described by spherical tensor operators.

The theorem can be stated as follows:

$$
\left\langle j' m' || C^{k} || j m \right\rangle = \frac{\sqrt{(2j+1)(2j'+1)}}{k+1} \langle j' m' | j m \rangle \langle j' || C^{k} || j \rangle
$$

where $\left\langle j' m' || C^{k} || j m \right\rangle$ are the matrix elements of the spherical tensor operator $C^{k}$, $j$ and $j'$ are the total angular momenta of the initial and final states, $m$ and $m'$ are the magnetic quantum numbers, and $\langle j' m' | j m \rangle$ and $\langle j' || C^{k} || j \rangle$ are the Clebsch-Gordan coefficients.

The proof of the Wigner-Eckart theorem involves the use of the commutation relations for the angular momentum operators, as well as the properties of the Clebsch-Gordan coefficients. The theorem is a powerful tool in quantum mechanics, and it has many applications in various areas of physics, including nuclear and molecular physics.

In the next section, we will explore the implications of the Wigner-Eckart theorem for the study of central potentials in quantum mechanics.




#### 7.4a Legendre Polynomials and Associated Legendre Polynomials

Legendre polynomials and associated Legendre polynomials are two fundamental sets of polynomials in quantum physics. They are solutions to the Legendre differential equation, which is a second-order differential equation. The solutions to this equation are the Legendre polynomials, denoted as $P_\ell(x)$. The associated Legendre polynomials, denoted as $P_\ell^m(x)$, are a generalization of the Legendre polynomials and are solutions to a modified Legendre differential equation.

The Legendre polynomials have several important properties. For instance, they are orthogonal polynomials, meaning that the polynomials $P_\ell(x)$ and $P_\ell'(x)$ are orthogonal to all other Legendre polynomials. This property is crucial in quantum mechanics, as it allows us to express any function $f(x)$ in the interval $[-1, 1]$ as a series of Legendre polynomials:

$$
f(x) = \sum_{\ell=0}^{\infty} a_\ell P_\ell(x)
$$

where the coefficients $a_\ell$ are given by the orthogonality condition:

$$
a_\ell = \frac{1}{\sqrt{2\ell+1}} \int_{-1}^{1} f(x) P_\ell(x) dx
$$

The associated Legendre polynomials also have several important properties. For instance, they satisfy a recurrence relation:

$$
(\ell-m-1)(\ell-m)P_{\ell}^{m}(x) = -P_{\ell}^{m+2}(x) + P_{\ell-2}^{m+2}(x) + (\ell+m)(\ell+m-1)P_{\ell-2}^{m}(x)
$$

This recurrence relation is particularly useful in quantum mechanics, as it allows us to express the associated Legendre polynomials in terms of the Legendre polynomials and their derivatives.

The associated Legendre polynomials also satisfy a differential equation:

$$
\frac{d}{dx} \left[ (1-x^2)^{m/2} \frac{d}{dx} P_{\ell}^{m}(x) \right] + \ell(\ell+1) P_{\ell}^{m}(x) = 0
$$

This differential equation is a generalization of the Legendre differential equation and is crucial in quantum mechanics, as it allows us to express the angular momentum operator in terms of the associated Legendre polynomials.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the properties of the Legendre polynomials and associated Legendre polynomials, and explore their applications in quantum mechanics.

#### 7.4b Spherical Harmonics

Spherical harmonics are a set of functions that are solutions to the Laplace equation in spherical coordinates. They are defined as:

$$
Y_{\ell}^{m}(\theta, \varphi) = \sqrt{\frac{(2\ell+1)}{4\pi}\frac{(\ell-m)!}{(\ell+m)!}} P_{\ell}^{m}(\cos\theta) e^{im\varphi}
$$

where $\ell$ is the azimuthal quantum number, $m$ is the magnetic quantum number, $P_{\ell}^{m}(\cos\theta)$ are the associated Legendre polynomials, and $e^{im\varphi}$ is the complex exponential.

The spherical harmonics have several important properties. For instance, they are eigenfunctions of the angular momentum operator, meaning that the angular momentum operator acting on a spherical harmonic yields a multiple of the spherical harmonic:

$$
\hat{J}^2 Y_{\ell}^{m} = \hbar^2 \ell(\ell+1) Y_{\ell}^{m}
$$

$$
\hat{J}_z Y_{\ell}^{m} = \hbar m Y_{\ell}^{m}
$$

This property is crucial in quantum mechanics, as it allows us to express the angular momentum of a quantum system in terms of the spherical harmonics.

The spherical harmonics also satisfy a recurrence relation, similar to the one satisfied by the associated Legendre polynomials:

$$
(\ell-m-1)(\ell-m)Y_{\ell}^{m}(\theta, \varphi) = -Y_{\ell}^{m+2}(\theta, \varphi) + Y_{\ell-2}^{m+2}(\theta, \varphi) + (\ell+m)(\ell+m-1)Y_{\ell-2}^{m}(\theta, \varphi)
$$

This recurrence relation is particularly useful in quantum mechanics, as it allows us to express the spherical harmonics in terms of the associated Legendre polynomials and the complex exponential.

The spherical harmonics also satisfy a differential equation, similar to the one satisfied by the associated Legendre polynomials:

$$
\frac{d}{d\theta} \left[ \sin\theta \frac{d}{d\theta} Y_{\ell}^{m}(\theta, \varphi) \right] + \ell(\ell+1) Y_{\ell}^{m}(\theta, \varphi) = 0
$$

This differential equation is a generalization of the Laplace equation and is crucial in quantum mechanics, as it allows us to express the angular momentum operator in terms of the spherical harmonics.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the properties of the spherical harmonics and explore their applications in quantum mechanics.

#### 7.4c Quantum Mechanics of Angular Momentum

In quantum mechanics, angular momentum is a fundamental concept that is closely tied to the concept of spin. The total angular momentum of a system is the sum of the individual angular momenta of its constituent particles. This is represented mathematically as:

$$
\mathbf{J} = \sum_i \mathbf{L}_i + \mathbf{S}_i
$$

where $\mathbf{J}$ is the total angular momentum, $\mathbf{L}_i$ is the orbital angular momentum of the $i$th particle, and $\mathbf{S}_i$ is the spin angular momentum of the $i$th particle.

The orbital angular momentum is defined as the cross product of the position vector and the linear momentum:

$$
\mathbf{L}_i = \mathbf{r}_i \times \mathbf{p}_i
$$

The spin angular momentum, on the other hand, is an intrinsic property of the particle and is not directly related to its motion. It is a vector quantity, but unlike the orbital angular momentum, it does not have a well-defined direction. Instead, it is described by two quantum numbers: the spin quantum number $m_s$ and the spin angular momentum quantum number $s$.

The total angular momentum operator $\mathbf{J}$ is also a vector operator, and it satisfies the commutation relations:

$$
[\mathbf{J}_x, \mathbf{J}_y] = i\hbar \mathbf{J}_z, \quad [\mathbf{J}_y, \mathbf{J}_z] = i\hbar \mathbf{J}_x, \quad [\mathbf{J}_z, \mathbf{J}_x] = i\hbar \mathbf{J}_y
$$

These commutation relations are analogous to those of the orbital angular momentum operator, and they are crucial in quantum mechanics, as they allow us to express the total angular momentum in terms of the individual angular momenta.

In the context of central potentials, the angular momentum plays a crucial role. The angular momentum operator commutes with the Hamiltonian, which means that the angular momentum is conserved in a central potential. This leads to the quantization of the angular momentum, which is a key feature of quantum mechanics.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the quantum mechanics of angular momentum and explore its implications for central potentials.




#### 7.5a Hydrogen Atom and Schrödinger Equation

The hydrogen atom, being the simplest atom in nature, has been a subject of intense study since the early days of quantum mechanics. The Schrödinger equation, a fundamental equation in quantum mechanics, can be solved exactly for the hydrogen atom, providing valuable insights into the quantum nature of atoms.

The Schrödinger equation for the hydrogen atom can be written as:

$$
-\frac{\hbar^2}{2\mu} \frac{1}{r^2} \frac{d}{dr} \left( r^2 \frac{d\psi}{dr} \right) + \frac{1}{n^2} = E
$$

where $\hbar$ is the reduced Planck's constant, $\mu$ is the reduced mass of the hydrogen nucleus and electron, $r$ is the radial distance, $\psi$ is the wave function of the electron, $n$ is the principal quantum number, and $E$ is the energy of the electron.

The solutions to this equation are the hydrogen orbitals, which are wave functions that describe the probability of finding the electron at a certain distance from the nucleus. These orbitals are characterized by three quantum numbers: the principal quantum number $n$, the azimuthal quantum number $l$, and the magnetic quantum number $m$.

The principal quantum number $n$ determines the energy of the electron. The larger the value of $n$, the higher the energy of the electron. The energy levels are quantized, meaning that the electron can only have certain discrete energy values.

The azimuthal quantum number $l$ determines the shape of the orbital. For $l=0$, the orbital is spherical and is called the s-orbital. For $l=1$, the orbital is dumbbell-shaped and is called the p-orbital. For $l=2$, the orbital is football-shaped and is called the d-orbital.

The magnetic quantum number $m$ determines the orientation of the orbital in space. For a given value of $l$, there are $2l+1$ possible values of $m$, each corresponding to a different orientation of the orbital.

The solutions to the Schrödinger equation for the hydrogen atom also provide a basis for the solutions of the Schrödinger equation for more complex atoms. This is because the hydrogen atom can be considered as a one-electron system, and the solutions of the Schrödinger equation for a one-electron system can be combined to form the solutions for a multi-electron system.

In the next section, we will explore the properties of the hydrogen orbitals and their implications for the behavior of electrons in atoms.

#### 7.5b Hydrogen Atom and Quantum Numbers

The quantum numbers $n$, $l$, and $m$ play a crucial role in determining the properties of the hydrogen atom. These numbers are integers and are subject to certain constraints. The principal quantum number $n$ must be greater than or equal to 1. The azimuthal quantum number $l$ must be less than or equal to $n-1$. The magnetic quantum number $m$ must be an integer between $-l$ and $l$ inclusive.

The principal quantum number $n$ determines the energy of the electron. The larger the value of $n$, the higher the energy of the electron. The energy levels are quantized, meaning that the electron can only have certain discrete energy values. The energy levels are given by the equation:

$$
E_n = -\frac{13.6}{n^2} \text{ eV}
$$

where $E_n$ is the energy of the electron in the nth energy level, and $n$ is the principal quantum number.

The azimuthal quantum number $l$ determines the shape of the orbital. For $l=0$, the orbital is spherical and is called the s-orbital. For $l=1$, the orbital is dumbbell-shaped and is called the p-orbital. For $l=2$, the orbital is football-shaped and is called the d-orbital. The shape of the orbital is determined by the spherical harmonics, which are functions of the angles $\theta$ and $\phi$ in spherical coordinates.

The magnetic quantum number $m$ determines the orientation of the orbital in space. For a given value of $l$, there are $2l+1$ possible values of $m$, each corresponding to a different orientation of the orbital. The orientation of the orbital is determined by the projection of the orbital onto the $z$-axis.

In the next section, we will explore the properties of the hydrogen orbitals and their implications for the behavior of electrons in atoms.

#### 7.5c Hydrogen Atom and Orbitals

The orbitals of the hydrogen atom are regions in space where the probability of finding the electron is high. These orbitals are determined by the wave function of the electron, which is a solution to the Schrödinger equation for the hydrogen atom. The wave function is a complex-valued function, and the square of its absolute value, $|\psi|^2$, gives the probability density of finding the electron at a given point in space.

The orbitals of the hydrogen atom are classified into three types: s-orbitals, p-orbitals, and d-orbitals. The type of orbital is determined by the azimuthal quantum number $l$. For $l=0$, the orbital is spherical and is called the s-orbital. For $l=1$, the orbital is dumbbell-shaped and is called the p-orbital. For $l=2$, the orbital is football-shaped and is called the d-orbital.

The shape of the orbital is determined by the spherical harmonics, which are functions of the angles $\theta$ and $\phi$ in spherical coordinates. The spherical harmonics for $l=0$, $l=1$, and $l=2$ are given by:

$$
Y_0^0(\theta, \phi) = s(\theta, \phi) = \frac{1}{2} \sqrt{\frac{1}{\pi}}
$$

$$
Y_1^{-1}(\theta, \phi) = p_y(\theta, \phi) = i \sqrt{\frac{1}{2}} \sqrt{\frac{3}{\pi}} \cdot \frac{y}{r}
$$

$$
Y_1^0(\theta, \phi) = p_z(\theta, \phi) = \sqrt{\frac{1}{2}} \sqrt{\frac{3}{\pi}} \cdot \frac{z}{r}
$$

$$
Y_1^1(\theta, \phi) = p_x(\theta, \phi) = \sqrt{\frac{1}{2}} \sqrt{\frac{3}{\pi}} \cdot \frac{x}{r}
$$

$$
Y_2^{-2}(\theta, \phi) = d_{xy}(\theta, \phi) = i \sqrt{\frac{1}{2}} \sqrt{\frac{5}{\pi}} \cdot \frac{xy}{r^2}
$$

$$
Y_2^{-1}(\theta, \phi) = d_{yz}(\theta, \phi) = \frac{1}{2} \sqrt{\frac{15}{\pi}} \cdot \frac{y \cdot z}{r^2}
$$

$$
Y_2^0(\theta, \phi) = d_{z^2}(\theta, \phi) = \frac{1}{2} \sqrt{\frac{15}{\pi}} \cdot \frac{z^2}{r^2}
$$

$$
Y_2^1(\theta, \phi) = d_{xz}(\theta, \phi) = \frac{1}{2} \sqrt{\frac{15}{\pi}} \cdot \frac{x \cdot z}{r^2}
$$

$$
Y_2^2(\theta, \phi) = d_{xx}(\theta, \phi) = \frac{1}{2} \sqrt{\frac{15}{\pi}} \cdot \frac{x^2}{r^2}
$$

The orbitals are also characterized by the quantum number $m$, which determines the orientation of the orbital in space. For a given value of $l$, there are $2l+1$ possible values of $m$, each corresponding to a different orientation of the orbital. The orientation of the orbital is determined by the projection of the orbital onto the $z$-axis.

In the next section, we will explore the properties of the hydrogen orbitals and their implications for the behavior of electrons in atoms.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have delved into the fascinating world of quantum physics, specifically focusing on angular momentum and central potentials. We have explored the fundamental principles that govern the behavior of particles at the quantum level, and how these principles apply to the concept of angular momentum. We have also examined the role of central potentials in quantum systems, and how they influence the behavior of particles.

We have learned that angular momentum is a fundamental concept in quantum physics, and it is described by the angular momentum operator. This operator is a vector operator, and it acts on the wave function of a particle. We have also learned that central potentials play a crucial role in quantum systems, particularly in the case of atoms and molecules. Central potentials determine the energy levels of particles in a quantum system, and they are responsible for the discrete energy levels observed in atoms and molecules.

In conclusion, the study of angular momentum and central potentials is crucial for understanding the behavior of particles at the quantum level. These concepts are fundamental to the understanding of quantum physics, and they provide the basis for many of the phenomena observed in quantum systems.

## Chapter: Chapter 8: The Hydrogen Atom

### Introduction

The hydrogen atom, being the simplest atom in nature, has been a subject of intense study since the early days of quantum physics. It is a fundamental system that provides a basis for understanding more complex atoms and molecules. This chapter will delve into the quantum mechanical properties of the hydrogen atom, exploring its energy levels, orbitals, and the concept of quantum numbers.

The hydrogen atom consists of a single proton in the nucleus and a single electron orbiting around it. The behavior of this electron is governed by the Schrödinger equation, which is a cornerstone of quantum mechanics. The solutions to this equation for the hydrogen atom result in a set of discrete energy levels, each associated with a specific orbital. These energy levels are quantized, meaning that the electron can only have certain specific energies.

The concept of quantum numbers is central to understanding the behavior of the electron in the hydrogen atom. These numbers, denoted as $n$, $l$, and $m$, determine the energy level, shape, and orientation of the electron's orbital. The principal quantum number $n$ determines the energy level, with higher values of $n$ corresponding to higher energy levels. The azimuthal quantum number $l$ determines the shape of the orbital, with values of $l$ ranging from 0 to $n-1$. The magnetic quantum number $m$ determines the orientation of the orbital in space.

In this chapter, we will explore these concepts in detail, providing a solid foundation for understanding more complex quantum systems. We will also discuss the experimental evidence that supports these quantum mechanical predictions, further reinforcing the validity of quantum physics. By the end of this chapter, you should have a solid understanding of the quantum properties of the hydrogen atom and be well-equipped to explore more complex quantum systems.




#### 7.6a Energy Levels Diagram

The energy levels diagram is a graphical representation of the energy levels of an atom or molecule. It is a useful tool for visualizing the energy states of an atom and the transitions between them. The energy levels diagram is particularly useful in quantum physics, where the energy levels of an atom are quantized, meaning they can only take on certain discrete values.

The energy levels diagram is typically represented with the energy levels on the y-axis and the quantum numbers on the x-axis. The energy levels are represented by horizontal lines, with the lowest energy level at the bottom and increasing energy levels moving upwards. The quantum numbers are represented by vertical lines, with the principal quantum number $n$ on the left, the azimuthal quantum number $l$ in the middle, and the magnetic quantum number $m$ on the right.

The energy levels diagram also includes arrows representing transitions between energy levels. These transitions occur when an electron absorbs or emits energy, changing its energy level. The energy of the transition is represented by the length of the arrow, and the direction of the arrow indicates whether the electron absorbs energy (moving upwards) or emits energy (moving downwards).

The energy levels diagram is a powerful tool for understanding the behavior of atoms and molecules. It allows us to visualize the energy states of an atom and the transitions between them, providing insights into the quantum nature of atoms. In the next section, we will explore the energy levels diagram in more detail, focusing on the energy levels of the hydrogen atom and the transitions between them.

#### 7.6b Quantum Numbers and Energy Levels

The quantum numbers $n$, $l$, and $m$ play a crucial role in determining the energy levels of an atom. The principal quantum number $n$ determines the energy level of the electron, with higher values of $n$ corresponding to higher energy levels. The azimuthal quantum number $l$ determines the shape of the electron's orbital, and the magnetic quantum number $m$ determines the orientation of the orbital in space.

The energy levels of an atom are quantized, meaning they can only take on certain discrete values. These values are determined by the quantum numbers $n$, $l$, and $m$. The energy of an electron in an atom can be calculated using the following equation:

$$
E_n = -\frac{13.6}{n^2} \text{ eV}
$$

where $E_n$ is the energy of the electron in the nth energy level, and $n$ is the principal quantum number. This equation is known as the Rydberg formula, and it provides a simple way to calculate the energy levels of an electron in an atom.

The energy levels of an atom are also affected by the presence of a magnetic field. In the presence of a magnetic field, the energy levels of an atom split into multiple sublevels. This phenomenon is known as the Zeeman effect, and it is described by the following equation:

$$
\Delta E = m \frac{eB}{2mc}
$$

where $\Delta E$ is the energy difference between the sublevels, $m$ is the magnetic quantum number, $e$ is the charge of the electron, $B$ is the magnetic field, $m_e$ is the mass of the electron, and $c$ is the speed of light.

The energy levels diagram provides a visual representation of these energy levels and transitions. The horizontal lines represent the energy levels, with the lowest energy level at the bottom and increasing energy levels moving upwards. The vertical lines represent the quantum numbers, with the principal quantum number $n$ on the left, the azimuthal quantum number $l$ in the middle, and the magnetic quantum number $m$ on the right. The arrows represent transitions between energy levels, with the length of the arrow representing the energy of the transition and the direction of the arrow indicating whether the electron absorbs or emits energy.

In the next section, we will explore the energy levels diagram in more detail, focusing on the energy levels of the hydrogen atom and the transitions between them.

#### 7.6c Hydrogen Atom and Energy Levels

The hydrogen atom, being the simplest atom in nature, serves as a model for understanding the energy levels of more complex atoms. The energy levels of the hydrogen atom are determined by the quantum numbers $n$, $l$, and $m$, as well as the presence of a magnetic field, as described by the Rydberg formula and the Zeeman effect.

The energy levels of the hydrogen atom are particularly important because they are the basis for the Balmer series, which describes the spectral lines emitted by hydrogen. The Balmer series is given by the equation:

$$
\frac{1}{\lambda} = R_H \left( \frac{1}{2^2} - \frac{1}{n^2} \right)
$$

where $\lambda$ is the wavelength of the emitted light, $R_H$ is the Rydberg constant for hydrogen, and $n$ is the principal quantum number. This series corresponds to transitions where $n$ is greater than 2, and the wavelengths of the emitted light are in the visible spectrum.

The Lyman series, which describes transitions where $n$ is less than 2, and the wavelengths of the emitted light are in the ultraviolet spectrum, is given by the equation:

$$
\frac{1}{\lambda} = R_H \left( \frac{1}{1^2} - \frac{1}{n^2} \right)
$$

The Paschen series, which describes transitions where $n$ is greater than 3, and the wavelengths of the emitted light are in the infrared spectrum, is given by the equation:

$$
\frac{1}{\lambda} = R_H \left( \frac{1}{3^2} - \frac{1}{n^2} \right)
$$

These series are named after the physicists who first studied them, Balmer, Lyman, and Paschen. They provide a way to predict the wavelengths of the spectral lines emitted by hydrogen, and they are a key part of the study of quantum physics.

In the next section, we will explore the energy levels of more complex atoms, and how they differ from those of the hydrogen atom.

This quantization of angular momentum has profound implications for the behavior of particles at the quantum level, and it is one of the key features that distinguishes quantum physics from classical physics.

## Chapter: Chapter 8: The Schrödinger Equation

### Introduction

The Schrödinger equation, named after the Austrian physicist Erwin Schrödinger, is a fundamental equation in quantum physics. It is a mathematical representation of the wave nature of particles, and it is the cornerstone of quantum mechanics. This chapter will delve into the intricacies of the Schrödinger equation, providing a comprehensive understanding of its principles and applications.

The Schrödinger equation is a partial differential equation that describes how the quantum state of a physical system changes over time. It is a wave equation, and it is used to calculate the probability of finding a particle at a certain position. The equation is given by:

$$
i\hbar\frac{\partial}{\partial t}\Psi(\mathbf{r},t) = \hat{H}\Psi(\mathbf{r},t)
$$

where $\Psi(\mathbf{r},t)$ is the wave function of the system, $\hat{H}$ is the Hamiltonian operator (representing the total energy of the system), $i$ is the imaginary unit, $\hbar$ is the reduced Planck's constant, and $\frac{\partial}{\partial t}$ is the partial derivative with respect to time.

In this chapter, we will explore the physical interpretation of the Schrödinger equation, its mathematical properties, and its applications in quantum physics. We will also discuss the concept of wave-particle duality, which is a key aspect of quantum mechanics and is closely tied to the Schrödinger equation.

The Schrödinger equation is a powerful tool in quantum physics, and it has been instrumental in the development of quantum mechanics. It has been used to explain phenomena such as quantum superposition and entanglement, and it has been the basis for many of the predictions and theories in quantum physics. Understanding the Schrödinger equation is therefore crucial for anyone studying quantum physics.

In the following sections, we will delve deeper into the Schrödinger equation, exploring its mathematical properties and its physical implications. We will also discuss some of the key applications of the Schrödinger equation in quantum physics. By the end of this chapter, you should have a solid understanding of the Schrödinger equation and its role in quantum physics.




#### 7.7a Virial Theorem and Central Potentials

The virial theorem is a fundamental principle in quantum mechanics that relates the average kinetic and potential energies of a system. It is particularly useful in the study of central potentials, where the potential energy is a function of the distance from the center of the system.

The virial theorem can be expressed mathematically as:

$$
2T = -n\frac{dV}{dr}
$$

where $T$ is the total kinetic energy of the system, $V$ is the potential energy, $r$ is the distance from the center of the system, and $n$ is the number of particles in the system.

In the case of central potentials, the potential energy is a function of the distance from the center of the system, and the force acting on each particle is directed towards the center. This results in a negative value for the derivative of the potential energy with respect to distance, as shown in the equation above.

The virial theorem can be used to derive the energy levels of an atom or molecule. For example, in the case of the hydrogen atom, the virial theorem can be used to derive the energy levels of the electron. The energy levels are given by the equation:

$$
E_n = -\frac{13.6}{n^2}
$$

where $E_n$ is the energy level, and $n$ is the principal quantum number. This equation is known as the Rydberg formula, and it accurately predicts the energy levels of the hydrogen atom.

The virial theorem is also useful in understanding the behavior of atoms and molecules. For example, it can be used to explain the stability of atoms and molecules. The virial theorem states that the average kinetic energy of a system is equal to half of the negative of the derivative of the potential energy with respect to distance. This means that in a stable system, the average kinetic energy is negative, indicating that the system is bound and stable.

In the next section, we will explore the implications of the virial theorem for central potentials in more detail. We will also discuss the concept of quantum confinement and its effects on the energy levels of atoms and molecules.

#### 7.7b Virial Theorem and Quantum Confinement

Quantum confinement is a phenomenon that occurs when the motion of particles is restricted to a small region of space. This can happen in various physical systems, but it is particularly relevant in the study of atoms and molecules. When an atom or molecule is confined to a small region of space, the energy levels of its electrons become quantized, meaning they can only take on certain discrete values.

The virial theorem plays a crucial role in understanding the effects of quantum confinement on the energy levels of atoms and molecules. As we have seen, the virial theorem relates the average kinetic and potential energies of a system. In the case of quantum confinement, the potential energy of the electrons becomes more dominant due to the confinement. This results in a decrease in the average kinetic energy of the electrons, leading to a decrease in the total energy of the system.

The virial theorem can be used to derive the energy levels of an atom or molecule under quantum confinement. For example, in the case of a hydrogen atom confined to a small region of space, the energy levels are given by the equation:

$$
E_n = -\frac{13.6}{n^2} - \frac{13.6}{a^2n^2}
$$

where $E_n$ is the energy level, $n$ is the principal quantum number, and $a$ is the confinement parameter. This equation shows that the energy levels become more negative as the confinement parameter $a$ increases, indicating a decrease in the total energy of the system.

The virial theorem also helps us understand the stability of atoms and molecules under quantum confinement. As the potential energy becomes more dominant, the average kinetic energy decreases, leading to a more stable system. This is particularly important in the study of atoms and molecules, as it helps us understand the behavior of these systems under different conditions.

In the next section, we will explore the implications of the virial theorem for the study of atoms and molecules in more detail. We will also discuss the concept of quantum confinement and its effects on the energy levels of atoms and molecules.

#### 7.7c Virial Theorem and Quantum Mechanics

The virial theorem is a fundamental principle in quantum mechanics that relates the average kinetic and potential energies of a system. It is particularly useful in the study of quantum confinement, where the motion of particles is restricted to a small region of space. In this section, we will explore the implications of the virial theorem for quantum mechanics.

The virial theorem can be expressed mathematically as:

$$
2T = -n\frac{dV}{dr}
$$

where $T$ is the total kinetic energy of the system, $V$ is the potential energy, $r$ is the distance from the center of the system, and $n$ is the number of particles in the system. This equation shows that the average kinetic energy of the system is equal to half of the negative of the derivative of the potential energy with respect to distance.

In the case of quantum confinement, the potential energy of the electrons becomes more dominant due to the confinement. This results in a decrease in the average kinetic energy of the electrons, leading to a decrease in the total energy of the system. The virial theorem helps us understand this decrease in energy, as it shows that the potential energy becomes more dominant as the confinement parameter $a$ increases.

The virial theorem also helps us understand the stability of atoms and molecules under quantum confinement. As the potential energy becomes more dominant, the average kinetic energy decreases, leading to a more stable system. This is particularly important in the study of atoms and molecules, as it helps us understand the behavior of these systems under different conditions.

## Chapter: Chapter 8: The Hydrogen Atom

### Introduction

The hydrogen atom, the simplest atom in the universe, has been a subject of fascination and study for physicists for over a century. It is a fundamental system that provides a basis for understanding more complex atoms and molecules. In this chapter, we will delve into the quantum physics of the hydrogen atom, exploring its unique properties and the principles that govern its behavior.

The hydrogen atom is a two-body system, consisting of a single proton in the nucleus and a single electron orbiting around it. The behavior of this system is governed by the principles of quantum mechanics, which allow us to understand the discrete energy levels of the electron and the transitions between them. These transitions are responsible for the spectral lines that make up the hydrogen spectrum, which has been studied extensively since the 19th century.

We will begin this chapter by discussing the basic properties of the hydrogen atom, including its energy levels and the Schrödinger equation that describes its behavior. We will then explore the concept of quantum confinement, which is crucial for understanding the behavior of electrons in atoms. We will also discuss the concept of quantum superposition, which allows us to understand the behavior of electrons in multiple states simultaneously.

Finally, we will delve into the concept of quantum entanglement, a phenomenon that is particularly relevant in the context of the hydrogen atom. Quantum entanglement is a phenomenon where two or more particles become correlated in such a way that the state of one particle cannot be described without considering the state of the other particles, even if they are separated by large distances. This concept, which was once considered purely theoretical, has been experimentally verified in recent years, and it has profound implications for our understanding of the quantum world.

In this chapter, we will use the mathematical language of quantum mechanics to describe these phenomena. We will use the Dirac notation, where states are represented as vectors in a complex vector space, and operators act on these vectors to transform them. For example, the state of an electron in a hydrogen atom might be represented as $|\psi\rangle$, and the Hamiltonian operator, which describes the energy of the system, might be represented as $H|\psi\rangle$.

By the end of this chapter, you will have a deeper understanding of the quantum physics of the hydrogen atom, and you will be equipped with the tools to explore more complex systems in the future.




#### 7.8a Circular Orbits and Eccentricity

In the previous section, we discussed the virial theorem and its application in central potentials. Now, we will explore the concept of circular orbits and eccentricity in the context of quantum mechanics.

A circular orbit is a special case of an elliptic orbit where the eccentricity is zero. In other words, the orbiting object moves around the central point in a circular path. This is the simplest type of orbit and is often used as a starting point for more complex orbits.

The eccentricity of an orbit is a measure of how much the orbit deviates from a perfect circle. It is defined as the ratio of the distance between the foci of the ellipse to the length of the major axis. In the case of a circular orbit, the foci coincide and the eccentricity is zero.

The concept of eccentricity is particularly important in quantum mechanics, as it is used to describe the shape of the orbit of an electron around the nucleus of an atom. The eccentricity of the electron's orbit can be calculated using the formula:

$$
e = \sqrt{1 - \frac{b^2}{a^2}}
$$

where $e$ is the eccentricity, $a$ is the semi-major axis of the ellipse, and $b$ is the semi-minor axis.

In the case of a circular orbit, the semi-major axis and semi-minor axis are equal, and the eccentricity is zero. This results in a circular orbit.

The concept of eccentricity is also closely related to the concept of the Kepler orbit. The Kepler orbit is a special type of elliptic orbit where the eccentricity is equal to the square root of the ratio of the semi-major axis to the semi-minor axis. In the case of a circular orbit, the Kepler orbit reduces to a circular orbit with eccentricity equal to zero.

In the next section, we will explore the implications of circular orbits and eccentricity for the behavior of atoms and molecules. We will also discuss the concept of the Kepler orbit in more detail.

#### 7.8b Quantum Mechanics of Circular Orbits

In the previous section, we discussed the concept of circular orbits and eccentricity in the context of quantum mechanics. Now, we will delve deeper into the quantum mechanics of circular orbits, focusing on the Schrödinger equation and the concept of angular momentum.

The Schrödinger equation is a fundamental equation in quantum mechanics that describes the evolution of a quantum system over time. In the case of a circular orbit, the Schrödinger equation can be simplified to a one-dimensional radial equation. This is because in a circular orbit, the angular momentum of the orbiting object is conserved, and the potential energy is independent of the angular position.

The Schrödinger equation for a circular orbit can be written as:

$$
-\frac{\hbar^2}{2mr^2}\frac{d}{dr}\left(r^2\frac{d\psi}{dr}\right) + V(r)\psi = E\psi
$$

where $\hbar$ is the reduced Planck's constant, $m$ is the mass of the orbiting object, $r$ is the radius of the orbit, $\psi$ is the wave function of the orbiting object, $V(r)$ is the potential energy, and $E$ is the total energy of the system.

The concept of angular momentum is also crucial in understanding the behavior of particles in circular orbits. In quantum mechanics, angular momentum is quantized, meaning it can only take on certain discrete values. This is a direct consequence of the Schrödinger equation and the conservation of angular momentum in circular orbits.

The angular momentum of a particle in a circular orbit can be calculated using the formula:

$$
L = mvr
$$

where $v$ is the velocity of the particle. In a circular orbit, the velocity is constant, and therefore the angular momentum is also constant. This leads to the quantization of angular momentum, where the angular momentum can only take on certain discrete values.

In the next section, we will explore the implications of these concepts for the behavior of atoms and molecules. We will also discuss the concept of the Kepler orbit in more detail, focusing on its implications for the quantum mechanics of circular orbits.

#### 7.8c Quantum Mechanics of Eccentricity

In the previous sections, we have explored the quantum mechanics of circular orbits and the concept of angular momentum. Now, we will extend our understanding to the quantum mechanics of eccentricity.

Eccentricity is a measure of how much an orbit deviates from a perfect circle. In quantum mechanics, the eccentricity of an orbit can be calculated using the formula:

$$
e = \sqrt{1 - \frac{b^2}{a^2}}
$$

where $e$ is the eccentricity, $a$ is the semi-major axis of the ellipse, and $b$ is the semi-minor axis. In the case of a circular orbit, the eccentricity is zero, as the semi-major axis and semi-minor axis are equal.

The concept of eccentricity is closely related to the concept of the Kepler orbit. The Kepler orbit is a special type of elliptic orbit where the eccentricity is equal to the square root of the ratio of the semi-major axis to the semi-minor axis. In the case of a circular orbit, the Kepler orbit reduces to a circular orbit with eccentricity equal to zero.

The quantum mechanics of eccentricity can be understood by considering the Schrödinger equation for an elliptic orbit. Unlike the Schrödinger equation for a circular orbit, the Schrödinger equation for an elliptic orbit is a two-dimensional equation. This is because the potential energy and the angular momentum are no longer independent of the angular position.

The Schrödinger equation for an elliptic orbit can be written as:

$$
-\frac{\hbar^2}{2mr^2}\frac{d}{dr}\left(r^2\frac{d\psi}{dr}\right) + V(r)\psi = E\psi
$$

where $\hbar$ is the reduced Planck's constant, $m$ is the mass of the orbiting object, $r$ is the radius of the orbit, $\psi$ is the wave function of the orbiting object, $V(r)$ is the potential energy, and $E$ is the total energy of the system.

In the next section, we will explore the implications of these concepts for the behavior of atoms and molecules. We will also discuss the concept of the Kepler orbit in more detail, focusing on its implications for the quantum mechanics of eccentricity.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have delved into the fascinating world of quantum physics, specifically focusing on angular momentum and central potentials. We have explored the fundamental principles that govern the behavior of particles at the quantum level, and how these principles apply to the concept of angular momentum. We have also examined the role of central potentials in quantum systems, and how they influence the behavior of particles.

We have seen how the quantum mechanical angular momentum differs from its classical counterpart, and how this difference leads to some intriguing phenomena. We have also learned about the concept of central potentials and how they are used to describe the behavior of particles in a central force field.

The principles and concepts discussed in this chapter form the foundation for understanding more complex quantum phenomena. They are crucial for anyone seeking to understand the quantum world and its implications for our understanding of the universe.

## Chapter: Chapter 8: The Hydrogen Atom

### Introduction

The hydrogen atom, the simplest atom in the universe, has been a subject of fascination and study for physicists for over a century. Its simplicity and yet complexity make it an ideal system to explore the fundamental principles of quantum physics. In this chapter, we will delve into the quantum world of the hydrogen atom, exploring its structure, energy levels, and the phenomenon of spectral lines.

The hydrogen atom consists of a single proton in the nucleus and a single electron orbiting around it. The behavior of this electron, governed by the principles of quantum mechanics, is what we will be focusing on. We will explore the Schrödinger equation, a fundamental equation in quantum mechanics, and how it applies to the hydrogen atom. This equation will help us understand the energy levels of the electron and the spectral lines that are emitted when the electron transitions between these levels.

We will also delve into the concept of quantum states and how they are represented using wave functions. The wave function of the electron in a hydrogen atom, known as the hydrogen atom orbital, will be a key focus of our exploration. We will learn how these orbitals are classified based on their shape and orientation, and how they are used to describe the electron's position and momentum.

Finally, we will explore the phenomenon of spectral lines, which are unique to the quantum world. These lines, which correspond to specific energy transitions of the electron, are what give the hydrogen atom its characteristic spectrum. We will learn how these lines are generated and how they can be used to identify the presence of hydrogen in various forms.

In this chapter, we will use the mathematical language of quantum mechanics to describe the behavior of the electron in a hydrogen atom. This will involve the use of complex numbers, differential equations, and linear algebra. However, we will strive to present these concepts in a clear and accessible manner, making use of visual aids and real-world examples to illustrate the principles at work.

By the end of this chapter, you should have a solid understanding of the quantum world of the hydrogen atom, and be equipped with the tools to explore more complex quantum systems.




#### 7.9a Discovery of Spin

The discovery of spin is a pivotal moment in the history of quantum physics. It was first discovered in the context of the emission spectrum of alkali metals. In 1924, Wolfgang Pauli introduced a "two-valuedness not describable classically" associated with the electron in the outermost shell. This allowed him to formulate the Pauli exclusion principle, stating that no two electrons can have the same quantum state in the same quantum system.

The physical interpretation of Pauli's "degree of freedom" was initially unknown. Ralph Kronig, one of Landé's assistants, suggested in early 1925 that it was produced by the self-rotation of the electron. When Pauli heard about the idea, he criticized it severely, noting that the electron's hypothetical surface would have to be moving faster than the speed of light in order for it to rotate quickly enough to produce the necessary angular momentum. This would violate the theory of relativity. Largely due to Pauli's criticism, Kronig decided not to publish his idea.

In the autumn of 1925, the same thought came to Dutch physicists George Uhlenbeck and Samuel Goudsmit at Leiden University. Under the advice of Paul Ehrenfest, they published their results. It met a favorable response, especially after Llewellyn Thomas managed to resolve a factor-of-two discrepancy between experimental results and Uhlenbeck and Goudsmit's calculations (and Kronig's unpublished results). This discrepancy was due to the orientation of the electron's tangent frame, in addition to its position.

Mathematically speaking, a fiber bundle description is needed. The tangent bundle effect is additive and relativistic; that is, it vanishes if `c` goes to infinity. It is one half of the value obtained without regard for the tangent-space orientation, but with opposite sign. Thus the combined effect differs from the latter by a factor two (Thomas precession, known to Ludwik Silberstein in 1914).

Despite his initial objections, Pauli formalized the theory of spin in 1927, using the modern mathematical language of vector spaces and operators. This formalism allowed for a deeper understanding of the quantum mechanical properties of spin, and paved the way for further developments in quantum physics.

#### 7.9b Spin-Orbit Interaction

The spin-orbit interaction is a key concept in quantum physics, particularly in the study of atoms and molecules. It is a direct consequence of the discovery of spin and the Pauli exclusion principle. The spin-orbit interaction is responsible for the fine structure of atomic spectra, which is the splitting of spectral lines into multiple components.

The spin-orbit interaction is a result of the coupling between the spin angular momentum of an electron and its orbital angular momentum. This coupling leads to a shift in the energy levels of the electron, which results in the fine structure of the atomic spectra. The fine structure is particularly important in the study of atoms and molecules, as it provides information about the internal structure and dynamics of these systems.

The spin-orbit interaction can be understood in terms of the total angular momentum of the electron. The total angular momentum is the sum of the spin angular momentum and the orbital angular momentum. According to the conservation of angular momentum, the total angular momentum of the electron remains constant. However, the spin angular momentum and the orbital angular momentum can change independently. This leads to the splitting of the energy levels of the electron.

The spin-orbit interaction can be calculated using the total angular momentum operator, which is defined as:

$$
\mathbf{J} = \mathbf{L} + \mathbf{S}
$$

where $\mathbf{L}$ is the orbital angular momentum operator and $\mathbf{S}$ is the spin angular momentum operator. The spin-orbit interaction energy is then given by:

$$
\Delta E = \frac{\beta}{2} \mathbf{L} \cdot \mathbf{S}
$$

where $\beta$ is a constant that depends on the specific system under consideration.

The spin-orbit interaction is a key concept in quantum physics, and it has been extensively studied and applied in various fields, including atomic physics, molecular physics, and condensed matter physics. It provides a deeper understanding of the quantum mechanical properties of atoms and molecules, and it has been instrumental in the development of modern quantum physics.

#### 7.9c Spin-Orbit Torque

The spin-orbit torque is a phenomenon that arises from the spin-orbit interaction in quantum physics. It is a key concept in the study of spintronics, a field that aims to utilize the spin of the electron for information processing. The spin-orbit torque is responsible for the manipulation of the spin state of electrons, which is crucial for the operation of spintronic devices.

The spin-orbit torque is a result of the coupling between the spin angular momentum of an electron and its orbital angular momentum. This coupling leads to a transfer of angular momentum from the spin to the orbital motion, and vice versa. This transfer of angular momentum can be represented as a torque, which is a measure of the force that tends to rotate an object about an axis.

The spin-orbit torque can be understood in terms of the total angular momentum of the electron. The total angular momentum is the sum of the spin angular momentum and the orbital angular momentum. According to the conservation of angular momentum, the total angular momentum of the electron remains constant. However, the spin angular momentum and the orbital angular momentum can change independently. This leads to the transfer of angular momentum between the spin and the orbital motion.

The spin-orbit torque can be calculated using the total angular momentum operator, which is defined as:

$$
\mathbf{J} = \mathbf{L} + \mathbf{S}
$$

where $\mathbf{L}$ is the orbital angular momentum operator and $\mathbf{S}$ is the spin angular momentum operator. The spin-orbit torque is then given by:

$$
\mathbf{T} = \frac{\beta}{2} \mathbf{J} \times \mathbf{J}
$$

where $\beta$ is a constant that depends on the specific system under consideration.

The spin-orbit torque is a key concept in quantum physics, and it has been extensively studied and applied in various fields, including spintronics, condensed matter physics, and quantum computing. It provides a deeper understanding of the quantum mechanical properties of electrons, and it has been instrumental in the development of new technologies based on spintronics.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have delved into the fascinating world of quantum physics, specifically focusing on angular momentum and central potentials. We have explored the fundamental principles that govern the behavior of particles at the quantum level, and how these principles apply to the concept of angular momentum. We have also examined the role of central potentials in quantum systems, and how they influence the behavior of particles.

We have seen how the quantum mechanical treatment of angular momentum differs from the classical treatment, with the former allowing for the existence of quantum states that are not possible in classical mechanics. We have also learned about the concept of spin, a purely quantum mechanical property that plays a crucial role in the behavior of particles.

In the realm of central potentials, we have discovered how the Schrödinger equation can be used to describe the behavior of particles in a central potential, and how this leads to the concept of quantum orbits. We have also seen how the quantum mechanical treatment of central potentials can lead to phenomena such as quantum tunneling and the quantization of energy levels.

In conclusion, the study of angular momentum and central potentials in quantum physics provides a deeper understanding of the behavior of particles at the quantum level. It is a complex and fascinating field that continues to be a subject of active research.

The hydrogen atom consists of a single proton in the nucleus and a single electron orbiting around it. The interaction between the positively charged proton and the negatively charged electron is governed by the Coulomb force, which is one of the fundamental forces in nature. However, the behavior of the electron in the hydrogen atom is not governed by classical mechanics, but by the principles of quantum mechanics.

Quantum mechanics, a branch of physics that describes the behavior of particles at the atomic and subatomic level, provides a mathematical framework for understanding the behavior of the electron in the hydrogen atom. This framework is encapsulated in the Schrödinger equation, a fundamental equation in quantum mechanics that describes the wave-like nature of particles.

In this chapter, we will explore the quantum mechanics of the hydrogen atom, starting with the Schrödinger equation and the concept of wave-particle duality. We will then delve into the quantization of energy levels in the hydrogen atom, a phenomenon that is unique to the quantum world. We will also explore the concept of quantum states and how they are represented using wavefunctions.

We began by introducing the concept of angular momentum and its relationship with rotational motion. We saw that angular momentum is a vector quantity and is defined as the product of the moment of inertia and the angular velocity. We also discussed the conservation of angular momentum and how it applies to systems with no external torque.

Next, we delved into the concept of central potentials and how they are used to describe the potential energy of a system. We saw that central potentials are spherically symmetric and can be described by a potential energy function. We also discussed the Schrödinger equation for central potentials and how it can be used to determine the energy levels of a system.

Finally, we explored the concept of angular momentum in quantum mechanics. We saw that angular momentum is quantized and can only take on certain discrete values. We also discussed the operators that represent angular momentum and how they can be used to calculate the probability of a system having a certain angular momentum.

Overall, this chapter has provided a comprehensive understanding of angular momentum and central potentials and their role in quantum mechanics. By understanding these concepts, we can gain a deeper understanding of the behavior of particles and systems at the quantum level.
